<div id="review_115402875" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115402875" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1938544-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330120106p2/1938544.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115402875?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1938544-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Intellectual Historians, Psychology students, Freudians</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Michael by: </span>
      Richard Beyler
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1938544-michael?shelf=read-grad-student">read-grad-student</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1938544-michael?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1938544-michael?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115402875" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4595148529126099992">This is one of those "seminal" books that shows you why so much of Western thought is totally screwed up. The premises and logic of Freud's argument are utter nonsense from beginning to end, yet he somehow taps into a vein of unconscious imagery within the contemporary Zeitgeist that still resonates 80 years later. Certainly, for anyone studying the early 20th century, the ideas in here will seem eerily familiar; Freud isn't so much creating a new argument here as speaking aloud what was in ever</span>
  <span id="freeText4595148529126099992" style="display:none">This is one of those "seminal" books that shows you why so much of Western thought is totally screwed up. The premises and logic of Freud's argument are utter nonsense from beginning to end, yet he somehow taps into a vein of unconscious imagery within the contemporary Zeitgeist that still resonates 80 years later. Certainly, for anyone studying the early 20th century, the ideas in here will seem eerily familiar; Freud isn't so much creating a new argument here as speaking aloud what was in everyone's heads at the time.<br /><br />So, the basis of this little thought-experiment was Freud's concept of the struggle between eros and thanatos: the drive for pleasure and the drive for death. That much still may apply, one way or another. As Freud saw it, the frustration of the id's natural desire for pleasure and love led to neurotic obsessions with death and destruction through aggressive behaviors. That part of the argument is sensible enough that the publishers see fit to include it on the back cover. What they don't tell you is that all of this began at some objective point in prehistory when a gang of brother cavemen killed and ate their father in order to possess and gang-rape their mother. For real. Freud says that this actually happened, and he "proves" it by pointing to various ancient myths in various cultures that can be seen as allegories for this objectively real event. I'm sure most Freudians would say that this is a metaphorical event, which took place in the imaginations of ancient peoples and that we still carry the legacy of this concept, but Freud's rejection of Jung's "collective unconscious" forces him into a position like unto fundamentalist Christians, insisting that his myths must be based upon actual fact. <br /><br />Indeed, this text is largely intended as a refutation of Jung and other psycho-analysts of the time who were suggesting that something valuable might be found in spirituality. Freud used this horrific imagery to posit that all religious thought is based upon perversity and hatred. Certainly this resonated well enough with many of his contemporaries, and no doubt it still does. Underneath this is also an underlying argument that it is civilization itself which necessitates the death-drive and the existence of neuroses, again a common enough idea. Freud is not "anti-civilization," of course, and wants to believe that the eros-principle can be integrated into a healthy psyche without a complete return to nature, but this seems to contradict his own logic. Psychology has long-since abandoned Freud, but he remains an influential force in philosophy and social science, which needs to move on as well. I recommend this book primarily for its historical perspective, not for any actual insights.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4595148529126099992" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2151546526" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2151546526" />
    <a title="Zadignose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4319285-zadignose"><img alt="Zadignose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340768521p2/4319285.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2151546526?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zadignose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zadignose" href="/user/show/4319285-zadignose">Zadignose</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4319285-zadignose?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2151546526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4896171179133121065">Freud is a fascinating and provocative philosopher. I believe, in attempting to understand him, it is correct to regard him as a philosopher while it is an error to regard him as a scientist--and this is an error which he seems to have made several times himself. Freud seems to have greatly influenced my own thinking, and I believe he has also significantly influenced human culture so that his ideas are not easy to escape. This book in particular, it seems, contains many profound probable truths</span>
  <span id="freeText4896171179133121065" style="display:none">Freud is a fascinating and provocative philosopher. I believe, in attempting to understand him, it is correct to regard him as a philosopher while it is an error to regard him as a scientist--and this is an error which he seems to have made several times himself. Freud seems to have greatly influenced my own thinking, and I believe he has also significantly influenced human culture so that his ideas are not easy to escape. This book in particular, it seems, contains many profound probable truths, which are likely intermingled with several falsehoods, spurious conclusions (or semi-open-ended, tentative conclusions), and perhaps some misguided speculations. Nonetheless, I won't stop reading Freud or being influenced by him. His ideas require the reader to go beyond comprehension, to avoid too-easy acceptance, and to grapple with them skeptically. To merely know what he thought is relatively simple, e.g. he thought civilization requires the ego to be restrained in order to promote a communal bond, and this sets up a conflict between contrary instinctual urges and a resulting anxiety and obstacle to happy fulfillment, which has a parallel in the psyche of the individual whose superego punishes and controls the ego, turning its aggressive impulses against itself. As compelling as Freud's ideas are, the two criticisms I would level at him are: 1) His compulsion to whip his ideas to make them fit a systematic understanding is itself never entirely fulfilling, it results in an incomplete, slightly nebulous picture that is more conditional than he is sometimes willing to admit (not so much in this book, but elsewhere his drive to reach absolute understandings can carry him away), and 2) Despite his best efforts, and despite the revolutionary nature of some of his ideas, he still seems to be limited at times by his own inherited cultural prejudices, such as concepts about gender relations and those about differences between civilized and primitive cultures. Yet, it is always hard to be strongly critical of Freud, because he usually anticipates all objections and gives evidence that he has taken those objections into account.<br /><br />So, yes, this book is to be recommended, and I hope to hear what you, as a reader, take away from it. I believe your reading will be rewarded if you use it as a tool to provoke your own thinking into new directions, even if you diverge from Freud in various places. Freud seems to use his own writing as just such a tool--his writing is an analytical process, more so than it is a thesis--or such is the way I choose to approach it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4896171179133121065" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2687973056" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2687973056" />
    <a title="Goatboy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35178006-goatboy"><img alt="Goatboy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558106346p2/35178006.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2687973056?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Goatboy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Goatboy" href="/user/show/35178006-goatboy">Goatboy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2687973056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14375988453964371983">The first 50 pages are some of Freud's clearest and most straight forward writing, and also some of his darkest. Want a matter of fact summary of why Freud believes that we who exist within a civilization that we ourselves built around us will never find happiness? Want to understand how this trap we built around ourselves (both necessary and also fated to make us discontented) pretty much defines us as humans who need to live with each other? Talk about being stuck between a rock and a hard pla</span>
  <span id="freeText14375988453964371983" style="display:none">The first 50 pages are some of Freud's clearest and most straight forward writing, and also some of his darkest. Want a matter of fact summary of why Freud believes that we who exist within a civilization that we ourselves built around us will never find happiness? Want to understand how this trap we built around ourselves (both necessary and also fated to make us discontented) pretty much defines us as humans who need to live with each other? Talk about being stuck between a rock and a hard place!<br /><br />I think Freud gets a bit bogged down in trying to theorize the origin of guilt, and I still cannot get behind his myth of the primal horde and the killing of the "first father" as a source event.<br /><br />But there's much here in this short work to contemplate and appreciate.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14375988453964371983" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1722416283">

<div id="review_3009884565" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3009884565" />
    <a title="Supreeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64325592-supreeth"><img alt="Supreeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602673666p2/64325592.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3009884565?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Supreeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Supreeth" href="/user/show/64325592-supreeth">Supreeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64325592-supreeth?shelf=2019">2019</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3009884565" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6636118666824293886">Except the stages of psychosexual stages I didn't know much about Freud, and what I knew was pretty sick and unacceptable. In this particular book, Freud talks about Society as a whole entity capable of thinking and gives the treatment similar to that of an individual. While it is accessible and straightforward, I still feel like some stuff just went over my head. This means I probably have to fill my brain with prerequisite readings and finding the shortest one isn't going to work. </span>

<div id="review_2540678589" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2540678589" />
    <a title="Shyam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64369397-shyam"><img alt="Shyam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507300907p2/64369397.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2540678589?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shyam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shyam" href="/user/show/64369397-shyam">Shyam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182360" href="/book/show/132445.Civilization_and_Its_Discontents">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64369397-shyam?shelf=freud">freud</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2540678589" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14769598288973354019"><blockquote><i>Here one can bring in the interesting case in which happiness in life is sought mainly in the enjoyment of beauty, wherever it presents itself to our senses and out judgement—the beauty of the human forms and gestures, of natural objects and landscapes, of artistic and even scientific creations., this aesthetic approach to the purpose of life affords little protection against the sufferings that threaten us, but it can make up for much. The enjoyment of beauty has a special quality of feeling th</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText14769598288973354019" style="display:none"><blockquote><i>Here one can bring in the interesting case in which happiness in life is sought mainly in the enjoyment of beauty, wherever it presents itself to our senses and out judgement—the beauty of the human forms and gestures, of natural objects and landscapes, of artistic and even scientific creations., this aesthetic approach to the purpose of life affords little protection against the sufferings that threaten us, but it can make up for much. The enjoyment of beauty has a special quality of feeling that is mildly intoxicating. Beauty has no obvious use, nor is it easy to see why it is necessary to civilisation; yet civilisation would be unthinkable without it.<br /><br />Our attitude to the third source of suffering, the social source, is different. We refuse to recognise it at all . . . much of the blame for our misery lies with what we call our civilisation . . . and it was inferred that a suspension or a substantial reduction of its demands would mean a return to the possibilities of happiness.<br />__________<br />The views I have developed here were at first put forward only tentatively, but in the course of time they have taken such a hold on me that I can no longer think in any other way. In my view they are theoretically far more serviceable than any others one might entertain; they produce what we strive for in scientific work—a simple answer that neither neglects nor does violence to the facts.</i><br />__________<br />Some good and very interesting thoughts on civilisation, and, being Freud, sexuality.<br /><blockquote><i>What did convulse our being, Freud suggests in Thea astonishing footnotes on the first and last pages of section IV, was the experience, or rather the smell of sex before we adopted an erect posture. But our sexuality fell when we stood up. Both anal eroticism and olfactory stimulation were subjected to what Freud calls ‘organic repression’; the result of this ‘repression’ is our horror of excrements and, at least according to Freud, a repugnance at sex, a shame provoked in us by our genitals and a disgust at genital odours which is so strong in many people that it ’spoil[s] their enjoyment of sexual intercourse’. And what a loss this was! By the end of the last footnote in section IV, Freud has transformed man’s depreciation of the sense of smell in sex into the repression of ’the whole of his sexuality’. Nothing is stranger in Civilisation and Its Discontents than the erotically confessional footnotes—that is, those moments when the distinguished anthropological imagination of the text depends into. Footnote where it enjoys the fantasy of a mythic, prehistoric convulsing of our physical being in the passionate sniffing of a male on all fours . . . we are, both ontologically and phylogenetically, ‘coded’ for pleasures we can no longer legitimately enjoy.</i></blockquote><br />__________<br /><b>Civilisation and Its Discontents</b><br /><i>It is impossible to resist the impression that people commonly apply false standards, seeking power, success, and wealth for themselves and admiring them in others, while underrating what is truly valuable in life.<br /><br />One may be inclined to suppose that these great men are appreciated after all only by a minority, while the great majority have no interest in them.<br /><br />Another of my friends, whose insatiable thirst for knowledge has driven him to conduct the most extraordinary experiments and finally made him virtually omniscient, has assured me that in practising yoga one can actually arouse new sensations and universal feelings in oneself by turning away from the outside world, by fixing one’s attentions on bodily functions, and by breathing in special ways. Such sensations and feelings he would interpret as regressions to ancient conditions in the life of the psyche that have long been overlaid.<br /><br />We achieve most if we can sufficiently heighten the pleasure derived from mental and intellectual work. Fate can then do little to harm us . . . at present we can only say, figuratively, that they seem to us ‘finer’ and ‘higher’, but their intensity is restrained, when compared with that which results from the sating of crude, primary drives: they do not convulse our physical constitution. The weakness of this method, however, lies in the fact that it cannot be employed universally, as it is accessible only to the few. It presupposes special aptitudes and gifts that are not exactly common, not common enough to be effective.<br /><br />. . . the house a substitute for the womb—one’s first dwelling place, probably still longed for, where one was safe and felt so comfortable.<br /><br />The benefits of order are undeniable; it enables people to make the best use of space and time, while sparing their mental forces.<br /><br />Not all human beings are worthy of love.<br /><br />No person has unlimited quantities of psychical energy at his disposal, and so he has to accomplish his tasks through an expedient distribution of the libido. Whatever energy he expends on cultural aims is largely denied to the opposite sex: his constant association with men and his dependency on this association even estrange him from his duty as a husband and father. The woman therefore sees herself forced into the background by the claims of civilisation and adopts a hostile attitude to it.<br /><br />In addition to the tasks involved in restricting the drives—for which we are prepared—we are faced with the danger of a condition that we may can ’the psychological misery of the masses’.</i><br /><br /><b>'Civilized' Sexual Morality and Modern Nervous Illness</b><br /><i>I will take from it only what I need as a starting point for my own contribution.<br /><br />Characteristic features of the civilized sexual morality that dominates us are for him the extension of the demands made on women to the sexual life go men and the prohibition of any sexual intercourse outside monogamous wedlock. Of course, regard for the natural differences between the sexes makes it necessary to publish men’s lapses less rigorously, and os to admit a double standard for them. Yet a society that entertains such a double standard cannot carry ‘love of truth, honesty, and humanity’ beyond a certain narrow limit; it cannot help teaching its members to cloak the truth, to gloss over things, and to deceive themselves and others. Civilised sexual morality produces still more harmful effects by glorifying monogamy . . . <br /><br /><blockquote>At the same time the individual’s needs have grown; demands for the enjoyment of life have increased in all strata of society, and a degree of luxury hitherto unknown has spread to classes that were previously untouched by it. Irreligion, discontent, and covetousness have proliferated in broad sections of the population. The enormous expansion of communications, due to the world-wide telegraph and telephone networks, has entirely transformed the conditions of trade and commerce. Everything is done in haste, at a fever pitch. The night is used for travel, the day for business; even “holiday trips” put a strain on the nervous system. Great political, industrial, and financial crises carry this excitement into dare wider areas of the population than ever before. Interest in political life has become universal: tempers are inflamed by political, religious, and social struggles, party politics, electioneering, and the immense growth of trade-unionism; people are forced to engage in constant mental activity and robbed of the time they need for relaxation, sleep, and rest. Big-city life has become increasingly sophisticated and restless. The exhausted nerves seek recuperation in increased stimulation, in highly spiced pleasures, and the result is even greater exhaustion. Modern literature deals predominantly with the most ticklish problems, which stir up all the passions and encourage sensuality, hedonism, and contempt for all ethical principles and ideals. The reader is presented with pathological characters and with subjects that involve psychopathic sexuality, revolution, and other problems. Our ears are excited and over-stimulated by large doses of noisy, obtrusive music. Theatres captivate all the senses with their sensational productions. Even the plastic arts turn by preference to the repulsive, the ugly, and then exciting, and do not hesitate to place before our eyes, with revolting realism, the most hideous sights that reality has to offer. —W. Erb, Über die wachsende Nervosität unserer Zeit, 1893</blockquote><br />The sex drive—or more correctly, the sex drives, for analytic research shows that the sex drive is made up of a number of components or partial drives—is probably more highly developed in human beings than in most of the higher animals. It is at any rate more constant, having almost completely overcome the periodicity to which it is subject in animals. It puts huge amounts of energy at the disposal of cultural activity; this is a consequence of one particularly marked characteristic—its ability to exchange the originally sexual aim for another—which is no longer sexual but psychically related to the first—is called the capacity for sublimation.<br /><br />Its may be said that the task of mastering such a powerful impulse as the sex dive otherwise than by satisfying it is one that can call for the whole of one’s powers. Only minority succeed in mastering it by sublimation, by directing the instinctual forces away from their sexual aim and towards higher cultural goals, and even this minority probably succeeds only for a time, and least easily during the period of ardent youthful vigour.<br /><br />For all the means of contraception that have been invented so far reduce sexual enjoyment, disturb the finer sensitivities of both partners, or are even direct causes of illness.</i></blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="14769598288973354019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3137825400" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3137825400" />
    <a title="Morgan Blackledge" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3897817-morgan-blackledge"><img alt="Morgan Blackledge" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1281826346p2/3897817.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3137825400?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Morgan Blackledge" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Morgan Blackledge" href="/user/show/3897817-morgan-blackledge">Morgan Blackledge</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3137825400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1894028784471556006">Freud’s brilliant (if anything underrated) investigation of the conflict between the desire for individuality and the expectations of society. <br/><br/>The essay represents a long form exploration of the tension between the needs of the group (promoted via civilization) and the needs of the individual, which for civilized people plays out in a conflict between survival and sex drives. <br/><br/>Freud’s primary claim, in a nutshell is, people need civilization to keep from ripping each other to shreds, but it’s als</span>
  <span id="freeText1894028784471556006" style="display:none">Freud’s brilliant (if anything underrated) investigation of the conflict between the desire for individuality and the expectations of society. <br /><br />The essay represents a long form exploration of the tension between the needs of the group (promoted via civilization) and the needs of the individual, which for civilized people plays out in a conflict between survival and sex drives. <br /><br />Freud’s primary claim, in a nutshell is, people need civilization to keep from ripping each other to shreds, but it’s also a total buzz kill, because they end up fucking a lot less. <br /><br />And....well....you go ahead and argue that point if you want, but I’m going to sit that one out. <br /><br />I mean, don’t get me wrong, I’m with Steven Pinker in the pro-civilization camp.<br /><br />But I can’t really argue with Freud’s terse little observation. I don’t actually think they contradict. And I’m quite sure those two would be bros if they lived in the same time. <br /><br />As evidenced by the fact that Freud begins this very essay with an all out assault on religiosity. <br /><br />Freud opens his argument by taking down his then pal C. G. Jung, by doubting the efficacy of Jung’s seemingly benign proto-new-age notions of ‘oceanic awareness’ as the fundament of religion. <br /><br />It’s obvious that there was something REALLY important that Freud just didn’t get, that Jung clearly did.<br /><br />But still. <br /><br />The argument is sobering and counters the sloppy brand of spiritual thinking Jungian’s commonly try to pass as not just true but ‘so true’. <br /><br />As in “that’s so true man”.<br /><br />In short, Freud employs the ‘explain it to me like I’m a dummy’ technique in regards to the oceanic experience, and in the end, shrugs it off by essentially saying that he doesn’t get it. <br /><br />Touché<br /><br />I’m quite sure Freud did understand what Jung was getting at. If only because Freud discusses adopting a diffused, global witnessing ego in the practice of psychoanalysis.<br /><br />I think Freud was simply rejecting the religious aspects of Jung’s claim, and challenging Jung for a kind of clarity that Jung legitimately did not posses.<br /><br />Freud soon leaves Jung behind and takes aim at Judeo Christian (J/C) ethical precepts. <br /><br />Freud takes particular umbrage with the J/C injunctions to be nice to your enemies and don’t have sex.<br /><br />Again. Freud plays dumb and playfully asks why one would ever do such a thing.<br /><br />He expands the argument by deconstructing the “infantile” father figure deity as a ‘sky daddy’ (my language not his) defense against life’s inevitable anxieties, particularly those concerning death. <br /><br />Then he shifts gears into what is, in my opinion, a really darn compelling argument for the existence of a death instinct. <br /><br />Which from the outside, not having read the source text, always seemed ridiculous to me, but after reading Freud’s humble (yes humble) and highly persuasive handling of the idea. <br /><br />I have to say. <br /><br />It’s a useful construct. <br /><br />The death instinct may not literally be ‘true’ in an objective, falsifiable sense, but it certainly seems to be extant in a subjective, functional sense. <br /><br />The trans-cultural fascination with death and violent imagery seems at least as present and powerful as sexual and erotic imagery.<br /><br />When understood as the impulse to aggression, you simply can’t deny the construct. <br /><br />When understood in terms of suicidal ideation and behaviors, in the form of the passive ‘desire to cease to exist’, or in the the literal drive to end ones own life, if for no other reason than to end the pain. <br /><br />Again. <br /><br />There is no lack of compelling evidence for all of this. <br /><br />Anyway. <br /><br />All that atheistic death talk really turned my crank. <br /><br />And considering the text in it’s historical context, i.e. it was written by a Jew, in Germany, in 1930, just makes you say WOW!<br /><br />Now that’s what I call a transgressive text. <br /><br />Remember, they didn’t have ‘safe spaces’ back then. <br /><br />Like WAY not. <br /><br />Finally.<br /><br />This is a dense, sometimes confusing read. <br /><br />So. <br /><br />Much if not all of what I have to say here may be incomplete and/or completely off base. <br /><br />There is much about this text that would take seriously rigorous scholarship to fully understand. <br /><br />And I’m simply not the one. <br /><br />That being said. <br /><br />After reading it. <br /><br />At minimum, I’m left with a deepening respect for Freud’s genius, charm and lasting influence.<br /><br />Great read 👍</span>
  <a data-text-id="1894028784471556006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1962093732?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mohamedridha Alaskari محمد رضا العسكري" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mohamedridha Alaskari محمد رضا العسكري" href="/user/show/7138289-mohamedridha-alaskari">Mohamedridha Alaskari محمد رضا العسكري</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182360" href="/book/show/132445.Civilization_and_Its_Discontents">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7138289-mohamedridha-alaskari?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1962093732" class="readable"

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2498555337" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2498555337" />
    <a title="Xander" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60074558-xander"><img alt="Xander" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490713726p2/60074558.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2498555337?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Xander" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Xander" href="/user/show/60074558-xander">Xander</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182360" href="/book/show/132445.Civilization_and_Its_Discontents">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2498555337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7843992219166120002"><i>"The fateful question for the human race seems to be whether, and to what extent, the development of its civilization will manage to overcome the disturbance of communal life caused by the human drive for aggression and self-destruction. Perhaps in this context the present age is worthy of special interest. Human beings have made such strides in controlling the forces of nature that, with the help of these forces, they will have no difficulty in exterminating one another, down to the last man. T</i></span>
  <span id="freeText7843992219166120002" style="display:none"><i>"The fateful question for the human race seems to be whether, and to what extent, the development of its civilization will manage to overcome the disturbance of communal life caused by the human drive for aggression and self-destruction. Perhaps in this context the present age is worthy of special interest. Human beings have made such strides in controlling the forces of nature that, with the help of these forces, they will have no difficulty in exterminating one another, down to the last man. They know this, and it is this knowledge that accounts for much of their present disquiet, unhappiness and anxiety. And now it is to be expected that the other of the two 'heavenly powers', immortal Eros, will try to assert himself in the struggle with his equally immortal adversary. But who can foresee the outcome?" (p.82)</i><br /><br />The above citation is the last paragraph of Civilization and its Discontents, written by Sigmund Freud, in 1930. Merely ten years later man would unleash the 'forces of nature' to self-destruct - Zyklon B, the Atomic Bomb, the administrative apparatus to run an Empire-wide work camp. World War II - or rather: nazism and communism - were the manifestions of Freud's main theses in the essay on Civilization. Admittedly, when he wrote it, he was already aware, being a Jew living in the German sphere of influence, of the direction the world was moving towards. Yet no one could have foreseen the horrors that lay waiting for mankind....<br /><br />In Civilization and its Discontents (1930), Freud tries to answer the question of 'what use is civilization?' seeing how it destroys our happiness en masse. There has to be some sort of utility, or else we all would choose happinness today. This is definitely an interesting question to try to answer, independent of whether one agrees or disagrees with the premiss that 'civilization causes us unhappiness'. <br /><br />Having read most of Freud's works, I can safely state that he was a genius in that he dared to ask penetrating, and highly interesting, questions. The way he worked out his answers in preliminary theories is a little bit too un-scientific for my taste, but coming up with interesting questions is as big a part of science - or life in general - as trying to answer them.<br /><br />First, the premiss. Freud claims human beings want to be happy and avoid unhappiness. This is called the 'pleasure principle', which basically governs our entire mental life, and through this our everday behaviour and emotions. And we are happy when we can fully give into our drives. The more these drives are restrained - either internal through the commandeering Super-ego or external through the fear of the loss of love from another human being - the less happy we are. <br /><br />The problem here is that our drives are antagonistic. Freud's view on this can almost be called <br />'secular Zoroastrianism', since he claims we have two drives: the sex drive and the death drive. The death drive came, evolutionarily speaking, before the sex driving. All animate matter wants to restore itself to its prior state as inanimate matter: all life wants to die. This death drive is bent on destruction, including self-destruction, constantly urges the Ego to let it express itself in the world: we want to behave agressively to everything and everyone. Besides this death drive, there's also the sex drive constantly urging our Ego to let it express itself: we desire sex objects - sex partners, preferably exclusively, at the cost of others - and are constantly looking for sexual gratification. So, in short, we have a destructive and a constructive drive working inside us, which both are constantly working to express themselves.<br /><br />Now, Freud's thesis about civilization creating unhappiness becomes clear: it is obvious that we don't see a Hobbesian world were each human being is fighting everyone else for the possession of sex objects. Civilization has, somehow someway, been able to control our drives and found some sort of compromise. We live together in communities, in nations even, and we care about people at the other side of the world (how much is an interesting question).<br /><br />So how did we manage this? And what is the cost of living civilized? But first, what is civilization?<br /><br />According to Freud, civilization is <i>"the sum total of those achievements and institutions that distinguish our life from that of our animal ancestors and serve the dual purpose of protecting human beings against nature and regulating their mutual relations." (p.27)</i><br /><br />Civilization thus means (1) mastery over nature through science, technology and economics, (2) mastery over the death and sex drives of all human beings belonging to the community. This latter part is crucial, since it's a precondition to the former (i.e. man has to work together to survive nature). Society forces all individuals to renunciate their agressive and sexual urges. It does this through various means: instituting justice (law); enforcing order and peace (police &amp; army); insitituting social contracts (no sex before marriage); etc. <br /><br />But even more importantly than enforced renunciation, society strives to make individuals internalize this renunciation of sex and agression. And here human nature offers a helping hand. The sex and death drives constantly fight our Ego to manifest themselves, but luckily the sex drives cannot work without the sex object. This means that we want to be loved by others (sexual love is here essential). This is check on both perverse sexual drives and our agressive urges. The fear of the loss of love is so strong, that we develop a sense of guilt. But this is only part of the story - it still means we behave ourselves as long as other people are watching. <br /><br />When we grow up, we enter into a conflict with our rival parent for the love of our mother/father. Eventually, we have to give up this ambiguous emotional battle and when this stage of the Oedipus-complex ends, our Super-ego forms. Part of our Ego identifies itself with the hated parent, and then this develops into a Super-Ego, which commandeers the Ego, so to speak. So when we enter adulthood, we are not only restricted by the others who are watching us behave, but we also feel internal guilt - this explains why we feel as bad about bad intentions as about bad deeds: the Super-ego sees these intentions rise from the Id, and is relentless in its threats to the Ego. <br /><br />In short: living together, in order to control nature's forces and ensure our survival, is made possible partly through the erection of institutions, partly through the fear of the loss of love, and partly through the existence of the Super-Ego. We are forced and threatened into not expressing our sex and death drives. <br /><br />Since mankind isn't perfect, the sex and death drives will from time to time be able to express themselves, leading to conflict - on small, and perhaps even on large scale. And not only this, living 'civilized' has its costs. If your constitution is flawed, repressed sexuality will lead to neurosis; and Freud saw increasing amounts of neurotic patients as a sign of increasingly repressed sexuality (which Freud had written about as early as 1908). But even if you're healthy, repressed sexuality leeds to bottled up frustration. Multiply this individual stored anger by the entire amount of people living in such a society, and you have masses waiting to explode. In other words: civilization has the effect of creating ever-increasing unhappy and angrier people; this store of 'energy' can be tapped by smart people for their own aims. Not suprisingly, Freud wrote about the civilized discontented in the era of communism, fascism and nazism. <br /><br />As I mentioned earlier, Freud asks the interesting questions, and for once he comes up with answers that common sense can grasp. Not that this says something about the truth of the answer, but still - compared with his theory of the Ego and the Id and the two primal drives, this essay is much more down-to-earth. <br /><br />Freud stays within the confines of contemporary scientific findings and his ideas about the duality of homo sapiens, its agressiveness as well as its need for love, have withstood the test of time. Nowadays we have done away with speculative theories of Eros, and we can explain humanity's agression and love as evolutionarily useful adaptations. In a sense, Freud's ideas in Civilization and its Discontents (1930) forms one of the starting points of Steven Pinker's The Better Angels of Our Nature (2011), which also claims homo sapiens can be tamed by the appropriate institutions and incentives. Pinker leaves aside the question of 'how much happiness such a civilized life costs', which is an interesting question in and of itself. <br /><br />I cannot recommend reading Civilization and its Discontents, though. The book is written in a very convulated style, and Freud draws on much of his earlier work - without knowledge of these earlier Freudian ideas, one will not fully grasp the main lines of thought in this essay. But Freud offers very interesting questions (as usual) and comes up with interesting answers that make you ponder your own life experiences (again, as usual), and this makes reading the book worth your while. Perhaps this emotional ambivalence is fitting the subject very well?</span>
  <a data-text-id="7843992219166120002" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_22281750" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22281750" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/101061-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1180332952p2/101061.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/22281750?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/101061-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone with an intrigued reaction to its title</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22281750" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17541984989903424574">It's impossible to read "Civilization and Its Discontents" and not come away with the impression that Freud is a genius. His ability to trace out cause and effect in human psychology is unparalleled. Most of his conclusions are convincing, and even the ones that aren't are at least thought-provoking.<br/><br/>The main weakness of this book is its desultory style. The first seven of its eight chapters read like an anthology of things Freud was thinking about this week, very loosely themed around the source</span>
  <span id="freeText17541984989903424574" style="display:none">It's impossible to read "Civilization and Its Discontents" and not come away with the impression that Freud is a genius. His ability to trace out cause and effect in human psychology is unparalleled. Most of his conclusions are convincing, and even the ones that aren't are at least thought-provoking.<br /><br />The main weakness of this book is its desultory style. The first seven of its eight chapters read like an anthology of things Freud was thinking about this week, very loosely themed around the source of civilization and its consequences for the psyche. It's not really until the last chapter that he weaves all the parts together. This last chapter is beautiful -- because you realize that all these threads really are all part of the same tapestry -- but it's also annoying. You're left thinking irritably, "Well if I'd known _that_ was where you were going with this I'd have read that part differently." This does not, however, affect the work's brilliance, only its accessibility.<br /><br />The book's thesis on civilization, as I understand it, can be summed up thus:<br /><br />Man has a constructive, or libidinal, impulse (Eros) and a destructive, or aggressive, impulse (death). Civilizations form when a group of people finds that they can more easily satisfy certain libidinal impulses jointly than they could apart. However, the aggressive impulse threatens to destroy any civilization thus formed. To avert this, civilization demands that its members restrain their aggression. The psyche responds by creating a "superego" that acts as an internal policeman. The superego is vested with the repressed aggression, which it directs against the ego in the form of guilt when the ego contemplates antisocial behavior. In excess, this causes neurosis.<br /><br />Civilization also demands that its members devote some of their energy to its upkeep. (We work jobs, pay bills, go to jury duty, etc.) But we have only a limited amount of energy. The energy we devote to "cultural aims" is energy we are not devoting to the pursuit of libidinal aims, especially sexuality. <br /><br />Thus civilization asks for abnegation not only of the aggressive impulse but also of the libidinal -- and this very abnegation prompts aggression towards society that then must be internalized. Guilt from this source is a civilizational malaise that Freud views to some extent as inherent in civilization, and not peculiar to our particular form of civilization.<br /><br />All of which speaks to me personally in a very direct way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17541984989903424574" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_21078429" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21078429" />
    <a title="Erik Graff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff"><img alt="Erik Graff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299888977p2/974210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21078429?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erik Graff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erik Graff" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff">Erik Graff</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/16204455-civilization-and-its-discontents">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Freud fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Erik by: </span>
      Edward Erickson
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/974210-erik-graff?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21078429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4533452257565453566">Many high school friends were a couple of years older. One of them, Ed Erickson, was particularly admired for his erudition, radicalism and interesting mother and sister. When he went away to the University of Illinois' flagship campus, I, still in high school, was honored to be invited to visit him on what amounted to a first overnight trip to a college.<br/><br/>Taking the Illinois Central downstate was an adventure in itself, another first. So, too, were the experiences of the ugly but enormous campus </span>
  <span id="freeText4533452257565453566" style="display:none">Many high school friends were a couple of years older. One of them, Ed Erickson, was particularly admired for his erudition, radicalism and interesting mother and sister. When he went away to the University of Illinois' flagship campus, I, still in high school, was honored to be invited to visit him on what amounted to a first overnight trip to a college.<br /><br />Taking the Illinois Central downstate was an adventure in itself, another first. So, too, were the experiences of the ugly but enormous campus set in the otherwise boring towns of Champaign and Urbana and the skyscraper dormitory he lived in. His roommate was a hippy! Students hung their food by strings from their windows in lieu of refrigerators!--a moat of frozen organic matter surrounded the building.<br /><br />The general library was remarkable as well. Its main reading room was underground, surrounded a submerged courtyard of sorts, the view not being much, just other seats across the way through floor-to-ceiling windows, just other students studying. Feeling self-consciously "too young", but proud to be there, I had a respectable copy of Freud's Civilization and its Discontents along, a book Ed had recommended and which I had found, cheap in paperback.<br /><br />Well, it was a quick read. Nothing complicated to it at all. Indeed, although I'd never read Freud before, I found nothing noteworthy or new within it. Psychoanalysis had become so much a part of the American popular culture that it was all old hat. The only thing that struck me at all was Freud's complaint about advances in transportation, about railroads hurrying us from nothing to nowhere.<br /><br />To impress Ed and to satisfy my conscience, I finished the dull thing and didn't read Freud again for years--after Ed introduced me to Jung in fact . . . </span>

<div id="review_705029793" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/705029793" />
    <a title="Peter (Pete) Mcloughlin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16212136-peter-pete-mcloughlin"><img alt="Peter (Pete) Mcloughlin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559007144p2/16212136.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/705029793?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter (Pete) Mcloughlin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter (Pete) Mcloughlin" href="/user/show/16212136-peter-pete-mcloughlin">Peter (Pete) Mcloughlin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393304510" href="/book/show/8433218-civilization-and-its-discontents">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16212136-peter-pete-mcloughlin?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16212136-peter-pete-mcloughlin?shelf=intellectual-history">intellectual-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16212136-peter-pete-mcloughlin?shelf=essays">essays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16212136-peter-pete-mcloughlin?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16212136-peter-pete-mcloughlin?shelf=european-history">european-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16212136-peter-pete-mcloughlin?shelf=1890-1959">1890-1959</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16212136-peter-pete-mcloughlin?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16212136-peter-pete-mcloughlin?shelf=modernism">modernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16212136-peter-pete-mcloughlin?shelf=religion-or-not">religion-or-not</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer705029793" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1157335771211744230">Freud as a scientist of the mind has long been regarded as a failure but as commentator on the human condition he is excellent. That is probably why his theories of the unconscious and the libido and the death drive have migrated from psychology to the English department. Freud's theories on human motivation make intuitive sense to many lay observers no matter how discredited psycho-analysis is in the scientific community and why his theories held sway among so many for a time.<br/> In "Civilization </span>
  <span id="freeText1157335771211744230" style="display:none">Freud as a scientist of the mind has long been regarded as a failure but as commentator on the human condition he is excellent. That is probably why his theories of the unconscious and the libido and the death drive have migrated from psychology to the English department. Freud's theories on human motivation make intuitive sense to many lay observers no matter how discredited psycho-analysis is in the scientific community and why his theories held sway among so many for a time.<br /> In "Civilization and Its discontents" Freud outlines the tragic realities of life for modern people. Human happiness and the order of the universe are imcompatible. What we call happiness more often is a reprieve from suffering that is human beings lot (might as well say any sentient being's lot) through a temporary satiation of desire. According to Freud the drives of a person have to be repressed to some degree to function in civilization and this leads to widespread dissatisfaction with modern life but happiness and human survival are not compatible at least in the modern world according to Freud hence the discontents of civilization. Freud outlines the two drives which propel people forward and must be repressed to some degree the libido and the death drive. The libido is well known, it is the pleasure principle but Freud after World War I came to recognize a kind of sadism and masochism in human nature a kind of cruelty he could not explain merely by the pleasure principle. Hence he put forward a death drive. This book shouldn't be taken as a definitive book on psychology but instead more the opinion of a wise old man as Christopher Hitchens explains in the forward.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1157335771211744230" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1271207066?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katie" href="/user/show/767960-katie">Katie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25130924-civilization-and-its-discontents">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1271207066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3561860220396887098">I do love me some Freud. His theories seem too speculative at times, but his insights on basic human psychology are enlightening. Although he spent most of the time trying to disabuse his readers of that ultimate "delusion," religion, I'm afraid it had the opposite effect on me. His expert construction of the ultimate human dilemma only strengthened my belief in and need for God, for which I thank him.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_612188'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/612188.Memories_Dreams_Reflections"><img alt="Memories, Dreams, Reflections" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1348081175l/612188._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TqoV+OOsjb6V5lMjihVvx39kQ8ES6MiAlJ1PXSu2XEYFsyOfvdcVJ8Ns1x+pkRCF9ls8KjomXIh6+Ymg4CvNvQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('GP3gqOCplnKxJsw2484e2EOxa3HRejaj2cmITEU0lPxT5NbPvtIO6+esSArASmGayo4Umvm0oqs3rU6xjqkFBw==')})
  }

Nietzsche rewrites the former as a history of cruelty, exposing the central values of the Judaeo-Christian and liberal traditions - compassion, equality, justice - as the product of a brutal process of conditioning designed to domesticate the animal vitality of earlier cultures. The result is a book which raises profoundly disquieting issues about the violence of both ethics and interpretation. Nietzsche questions moral certainties by showing that religion and science have no claim to absolute truth, before turning on his own arguments in order to call their very presuppositions into question. The Genealogy is the most sustained of Nietzsche\'s later works and offers one of the fullest expressions of his characteristic concerns. He shows how many intellectuals have denied the existence of human nature by embracing three linked dogmas: the Blank Slate (the mind has no innate traits), the Noble Savage (people are born good and corrupted by society), and the Ghost in the Machine (each of us has a soul that makes choices free from biology). Each dogma carries a moral burden, so their defenders have engaged in desperate tactics to discredit the scientists who are now challenging them.\n\nPinker injects calm and rationality into these debates by showing that equality, progress, responsibility, and purpose have nothing to fear from discoveries about a rich human nature. He disarms even the most menacing threats with clear thinking, common sense, and pertinent facts from science and history.\n\nDespite its popularity among intellectuals during much of the twentieth century, he argues, the doctrine of the Blank Slate may have done more harm than good. It denies our common humanity and our individual preferences, replaces hardheaded analyses of social problems with feel-good slogans, and distorts our understanding of government, violence, parenting, and the arts.\n\nPinker shows that an acknowledgement of human nature that is grounded in science and common sense, far from being dangerous, can complement insights about the human condition made by millennia of artists and philosophers. All this is done in the style that earned his previous books many prizes and worldwide acclaim: wit, lucidity, and insight into matters great and small.<\/span>\n  <a The last book of its kind, which was published more than 20 years ago, predated t<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13726026685971761868\" style=\"display:none\">This is the first text to come along in many years that makes psychoanalytic personality theory and its implications for practice accessible to beginning practitioners. The last book of its kind, which was published more than 20 years ago, predated the development of such significant concepts as borderline syndromes, narcissistic pathology, dissociative disorders and self-defeating personality.Contemporary students often react with bewilderment to the language of pioneering analysts like Reich and Fenichel and, since 1980, the various volumes of the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders (DSM) have reflected an empirical-descriptive orientation that deliberately eschews psychodynamic assumptions. Consequently, today\'s therapist in training may have little exposure to the rich clinical and theoretical history behind each disorder mentioned in DSM; to psychoanalytic expertise with widely recognized character patterns not mentioned in DSM, such as depressive and hypomanic psychologies, high-functioning schizoid personalities, and hysterical personalities; or to a comprehensive, theoretically sophisticated rationale that links assessment to treatment. Filling the need for a text that clearly lays out the conceptual heritage that psychoanalytic practitioners take for granted, this important new volume explicates the major clinically important character types and suggests how an appreciation of the patients\' individual personality structure should influence the therapist\'s focus and style of intervention. Dispensing with the dense jargon that often discourages people from learning, Nancy McWilliams writes in a lucid, personal manner that demystifies psychodynamic theory and practice. Innumerable clinical vignettes are presented with humor, candor, and compassion, bringing abstract concepts to life.Comprehensive in scope, Psychoanalytic Diagnosis will be valued by seasoned clinicians and students alike. Psychodynamically oriented readers will find it an excellent introduction to psychoanalytic diagnostic thinking. For those identified with other approaches, it will foster psychoanalytic literacy, providing them with the capacity to better understand the approaches of their analytically oriented colleagues. And yet it is complete in itself. For it is a work of art.&quot; —Walter Kaufmann in the Introduction\nNietzsche called The Gay Science &quot;the most personal of all my books.&quot; It was here that he first proclaimed the death of God—to which a large part of the book is devoted—and his doctrine of the eternal recurrence.\nWalter Kaufmann\'s commentary, with its many quotations from previously untranslated letters, brings to life Nietzsche as a human being and illuminates his philosophy. The book contains some of Nietzsche\'s most sustained discussions of art and morality, knowledge and truth, the intellectual conscience and the origin of logic.\nMost of the book was written just before Thus Spoke Zarathustra, the last part five years later, after Beyond Good and Evil. We encounter Zarathustra in these pages as well as many of Nietzsche\'s most interesting philosophical ideas and the largest collection of his own poetry that he himself ever published.\nWalter Kaufmann\'s English versions of Nietzsche represent one of the major translation enterprises of our time.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/10017.Sigmund_Freud">About Sigmund Freud</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/10017.Sigmund_Freud"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1406688955p3/10017.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/10017.Sigmund_Freud">Sigmund Freud
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
5,684
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16477656468774478402"><b>Dr. Sigismund Freud</b> (later changed to Sigmund) was a neurologist and the founder of psychoanalysis, who created an entirely new approach to the understanding of the human personality. He is regarded as one of the most influential—and controversial—minds of the 20th century.<br/><br/>In 1873, Freud began to study medicine at the University of Vienna. After graduating, he worked at the Vienna General Hospital</span>
  <span id="freeText16477656468774478402" style="display:none"><b>Dr. Sigismund Freud</b> (later changed to Sigmund) was a neurologist and the founder of psychoanalysis, who created an entirely new approach to the understanding of the human personality. He is regarded as one of the most influential—and controversial—minds of the 20th century.<br /><br />In 1873, Freud began to study medicine at the University of Vienna. After graduating, he worked at the Vienna General Hospital. He collaborated with Josef Breuer in treating hysteria by the recall of painful experiences under hypnosis. In 1885, Freud went to Paris as a student of the neurologist Jean Charcot. On his return to Vienna the following year, Freud set up in private practice, specialising in nervous and brain disorders. The same year he married Martha Bernays, with whom he had six children.<br /><br />Freud developed the theory that humans have an unconscious in which sexual and aggressive impulses are in perpetual conflict for supremacy with the defences against them. In 1897, he began an intensive analysis of himself. In 1900, his major work 'The Interpretation of Dreams' was published in which Freud analysed dreams in terms of unconscious desires and experiences.<br /><br />In 1902, Freud was appointed Professor of Neuropathology at the University of Vienna, a post he held until 1938. Although the medical establishment disagreed with many of his theories, a group of pupils and followers began to gather around Freud. In 1910, the International Psychoanalytic Association was founded with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/38285.Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung" rel="nofollow noopener">Carl Jung</a>, a close associate of Freud's, as the president. Jung later broke with Freud and developed his own theories.<br /><br />After World War One, Freud spent less time in clinical observation and concentrated on the application of his theories to history, art, literature and anthropology. In 1923, he published 'The Ego and the Id', which suggested a new structural model of the mind, divided into the 'id, the 'ego' and the 'superego'.<br /><br />In 1933, the Nazis publicly burnt a number of Freud's books. In 1938, shortly after the Nazis annexed Austria, Freud left Vienna for London with his wife and daughter Anna.<br /><br />Freud had been diagnosed with cancer of the jaw in 1923, and underwent more than 30 operations. He died of cancer on 23 September 1939.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16477656468774478402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
